intensity, notably that of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_first_overall_NBA_draft_picks" title="List of first overall NBA draft picks">number one draft pick</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2001_NBA_draft" title="2001 NBA draft">2001 NBA draft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kwame_Brown" title="Kwame Brown">Kwame Brown</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rob_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rob-141">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chemistry_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chemistry-142">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the recognition that 2002–03 would be Jordan's final season, tributes were paid to him throughout the NBA. In his final game at the <a href="/wiki/United_Center" title="United Center">United Center</a> in Chicago, which was his old home court, Jordan received a four-minute standing ovation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Miami_Heat" title="Miami Heat">Miami Heat</a> retired the number 23 jersey on April 11, 2003, even though Jordan never played for the team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup> At the 2003 All-Star Game, Jordan was offered a starting spot from <a href="/wiki/Tracy_McGrady" title="Tracy McGrady">Tracy McGrady</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allen_Iverson" title="Allen Iverson">Allen Iverson</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup> but refused both. In the end, he accepted the spot of <a href="/wiki/Vince_Carter" title="Vince Carter">Vince Carter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jordan played in his final NBA game on April 16, 2003, in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>. After scoring only 13 points in the game, Jordan went to the bench with 4 minutes and 13 seconds remaining in the third quarter and his team trailing the <a href="/wiki/2002%E2%80%9303_Philadelphia_76ers_season" title="2002–03 Philadelphia 76ers season">Philadelphia 76ers</a>, 75–56. Just after the start of the fourth quarter, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Union_Center" title="First Union Center">First Union Center</a> crowd began chanting "We want Mike!" After much encouragement from coach <a href="/wiki/Doug_Collins_(basketball)" title="Doug Collins (basketball)">Doug Collins</a>, Jordan finally rose from the bench and re-entered the game, replacing <a href="/wiki/Larry_Hughes" title="Larry Hughes">Larry Hughes</a> with 2:35 remaining. At 1:45, Jordan was intentionally fouled by the 76ers' <a href="/wiki/Eric_Snow" title="Eric Snow">Eric Snow</a>, and stepped to the line to make both free throws. After the second foul shot, the 76ers in-bounded the ball to rookie <a href="/wiki/John_Salmons" title="John Salmons">John Salmons</a>, who in turn was intentionally fouled by <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Simmons" title="Bobby Simmons">Bobby Simmons</a> one second later, stopping time so that Jordan could return to the bench. Jordan received a three-minute standing ovation from his teammates, his opponents, the officials, and the crowd of 21,257 fans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fg_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-fg-147">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National_team_career">National team career</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cazzaro_Jordan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="604" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Cazzaro_Jordan.jpg/220px-Cazzaro_Jordan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Cazzaro_Jordan.jpg/330px-Cazzaro_Jordan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Cazzaro_Jordan.jpg/440px-Cazzaro_Jordan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cazzaro_Jordan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jordan on the "Dream Team" in 1992</div></div></div>
<p>Jordan made his debut for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_men%27s_national_basketball_team" title="United States men's national basketball team">United States national team</a> at the <a href="/wiki/1983_Pan_American_Games" title="1983 Pan American Games">1983 Pan American Games</a> in <a href="/wiki/Caracas" title="Caracas">Caracas</a>, Venezuela. He led the team in scoring with 17.3 ppg as the U.S., coached by <a href="/wiki/Jack_Hartman" title="Jack Hartman">Jack Hartman</a>, won the <a href="/wiki/Gold_medal" title="Gold medal">gold medal</a> in the competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usbasket1983_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-usbasket1983-149">[147]</a></sup> A year later, he won another gold medal in the <a href="/wiki/1984_Summer_Olympics" title="1984 Summer Olympics">1984 Summer Olympics</a>. The <a href="/wiki/1984_United_States_men%27s_Olympic_basketball_team" title="1984 United States men's Olympic basketball team">team</a> was coached by <a href="/wiki/Bob_Knight" title="Bob Knight">Bob Knight</a> and featured players such as <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Ewing" title="Patrick Ewing">Patrick Ewing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sam_Perkins" title="Sam Perkins">Sam Perkins</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chris_Mullin_(basketball)" title="Chris Mullin (basketball)">Chris Mullin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Alford" title="Steve Alford">Steve Alford</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wayman_Tisdale" title="Wayman Tisdale">Wayman Tisdale</a>. Jordan led the team in scoring, averaging 17.1 ppg for the tournament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usbasket1984_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-usbasket1984-150">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1992, he was a member of the star-studded squad that was dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/1992_United_States_men%27s_Olympic_basketball_team" title="1992 United States men's Olympic basketball team">Dream Team</a>", which included <a href="/wiki/Larry_Bird" title="Larry Bird">Larry Bird</a> and Magic Johnson. The team went on to win two gold medals: the first one in the <a href="/wiki/1992_Tournament_of_the_Americas" title="1992 Tournament of the Americas">1992 Tournament of the Americas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usbasket1992a_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-usbasket1992a-151">[149]</a></sup> and the second one in the <a href="/wiki/1992_Summer_Olympics" title="1992 Summer Olympics">1992 Summer Olympics</a>. Jordan was the only player to start all eight games in the Olympics and averaged 14.9 ppg, finishing second on the team in scoring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usbasket1992b_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-usbasket1992b-152">[150]</a></sup> Team USA never lost a single game in the four tournaments in which Jordan played.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usbasket1983_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-usbasket1983-149">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usbasket1984_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-usbasket1984-150">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usbasket1992a_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-usbasket1992a-151">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usbasket1992b_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-usbasket1992b-152">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-retirement">Post-retirement</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MJ_golf_course.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2331" data-file-width="3481" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/MJ_golf_course.jpg/220px-MJ_golf_course.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/MJ_golf_course.jpg/330px-MJ_golf_course.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/MJ_golf_course.jpg/440px-MJ_golf_course.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MJ_golf_course.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jordan on a golf course in 2007</div></div></div>
<p>After his third retirement, Jordan assumed that he would be able to return to his front office position as Director of Basketball Operations with the Wizards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup> However, his previous tenure in the Wizards' front office had produced the aforementioned mixed results and may have also influenced the trade of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hamilton_(basketball)" title="Richard Hamilton (basketball)">Richard "Rip" Hamilton</a> for <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Stackhouse" title="Jerry Stackhouse">Jerry Stackhouse</a>, although Jordan was not technically Director of Basketball Operations in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pollin_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pollin-125">[123]</a></sup> On May 7, 2003, Wizards owner <a href="/wiki/Abe_Pollin" title="Abe Pollin">Abe Pollin</a> fired Jordan as the team's president of basketball operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pollin_125-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pollin-125">[123]</a></sup> Jordan later stated that he felt betrayed, and that if he had known he would be fired upon retiring he never would have come back to play for the Wizards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB-74">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jordan kept busy over the next few years. He stayed in shape, played golf in celebrity charity tournaments, and spent time with his family in Chicago. He also promoted his Jordan Brand clothing line and rode motorcycles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup> Since 2004, Jordan has owned Michael Jordan Motorsports, a professional closed-course motorcycle road racing team that competed with two <a href="/wiki/Suzuki" title="Suzuki">Suzukis</a> in the <a href="/wiki/AMA_Superbike_Championship" title="AMA Superbike Championship">premier Superbike championship</a> sanctioned by the <a href="/wiki/American_Motorcyclist_Association" title="American Motorcyclist Association">American Motorcyclist Association</a> (AMA) until the end of the 2013 season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Charlotte_Bobcats_.2F_Hornets"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Charlotte_Bobcats_/_Hornets">Charlotte Bobcats / Hornets</span></h3>
<p>On June 15, 2006, Jordan bought a minority stake in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charlotte_Bobcats" title="Charlotte Bobcats">Charlotte Bobcats</a> (now known as the <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Hornets" title="Charlotte Hornets">Hornets</a>), becoming the team's second-largest shareholder behind majority owner <a href="/wiki/Robert_L._Johnson" title="Robert L. Johnson">Robert L. Johnson</a>. As part of the deal, Jordan took full control over the basketball side of the operation, with the title "Managing Member of Basketball Operations".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup> Despite Jordan's previous success as an endorser, he has made an effort not to be included in Charlotte's marketing campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup> A decade earlier, Jordan had made a bid to become part-owner of Charlotte's original NBA team, the Charlotte Hornets, but talks collapsed when owner <a href="/wiki/George_Shinn" title="George Shinn">George Shinn</a> refused to give Jordan complete control of basketball operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2010, it was reported that Jordan was seeking majority ownership of the Bobcats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[159]</a></sup> As February wore on, it became apparent that Jordan and former <a href="/wiki/Houston_Rockets" title="Houston Rockets">Houston Rockets</a> president <a href="/wiki/George_Postolos" title="George Postolos">George Postolos</a> were the leading contenders for ownership of the team. On February 27, the Bobcats announced that Johnson had reached an agreement with Jordan and his group, MJ Basketball Holdings, to buy the team from Johnson pending NBA approval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup> On March 17, the NBA Board of Governors unanimously approved Jordan's purchase, making him the first former player to become the majority owner of an NBA team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup> It also made him the league's only African-American majority owner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/2011_NBA_lockout" title="2011 NBA lockout">2011 NBA lockout</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> wrote that Jordan led a group of 10 to 14 hardline owners who wanted to cap the players' share of basketball-related income at 50 percent and as low as 47. Journalists observed that, during the <a href="/wiki/1998%E2%80%9399_NBA_lockout" title="1998–99 NBA lockout">labor dispute in 1998</a>, Jordan had told <a href="/wiki/Washington_Wizards" title="Washington Wizards">Washington Wizards</a> then-owner <a href="/wiki/Abe_Pollin" title="Abe Pollin">Abe Pollin</a>, "If you can't make a profit, you should sell your team."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jason_Whitlock" title="Jason Whitlock">Jason Whitlock</a> of <a href="/wiki/FoxSports.com" title="FoxSports.com">FoxSports.com</a> called Jordan a "sellout" wanting "current players to pay for his incompetence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whitlock_11052011_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-whitlock_11052011-166">[164]</a></sup> He cited Jordan's executive decisions to draft disappointing players <a href="/wiki/Kwame_Brown" title="Kwame Brown">Kwame Brown</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adam_Morrison" title="Adam Morrison">Adam Morrison</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whitlock_11052011_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-whitlock_11052011-166">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/2011%E2%80%9312_NBA_season" title="2011–12 NBA season">2011–12 NBA season</a> that was shortened to 66 games by the lockout, the Bobcats posted a 7–59 record. The team closed out the season with a 23-game losing streak. Their .106 winning percentage was the worst in NBA history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[165]</a></sup> Before the next season, Jordan said: "I'm not real happy about the record book scenario last year. It's very, very frustrating."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 2019 NBA offseason, Jordan sold a minority piece of the Hornets to Gabe Plotkin and Daniel Sundheim, retaining the majority of the team for himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="23XI_Racing">23XI Racing</span></h3>
<p>On September 21, 2020, Jordan and <a href="/wiki/NASCAR" title="NASCAR">NASCAR</a> driver <a href="/wiki/Denny_Hamlin" title="Denny Hamlin">Denny Hamlin</a> announced they would be fielding a NASCAR team with <a href="/wiki/Bubba_Wallace" title="Bubba Wallace">Bubba Wallace</a> driving, beginning competition in the <a href="/wiki/2021_NASCAR_Cup_Series" title="2021 NASCAR Cup Series">2021 season</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup> On October 22, the team's name was confirmed to be <a href="/wiki/23XI_Racing" title="23XI Racing">23XI Racing</a> (pronounced twenty-three eleven) and the team's entry will bear the number 23.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Player_profile">Player profile</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="385" data-file-width="288" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg/170px-Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg/255px-Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c3/Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jordan_by_Lipofsky_16577.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jordan dunking the ball, 1987–88</div></div></div>
<p>Jordan was a <a href="/wiki/Shooting_guard" title="Shooting guard">shooting guard</a> who could also play as a <a href="/wiki/Small_forward" title="Small forward">small forward</a> (the position he would primarily play during his second return to professional basketball with the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Wizards" title="Washington Wizards">Washington Wizards</a>), and as a <a href="/wiki/Point_guard" title="Point guard">point guard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-br-31">[29]</a></sup> Jordan was known throughout his career as a strong clutch performer. With the Bulls, he decided 25 games with field goals or free throws in the last 30 seconds, including two NBA Finals games and five other playoff contests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[170]</a></sup> His competitiveness was visible in his prolific <a href="/wiki/Trash-talk" title="Trash-talk">trash-talk</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[171]</a></sup> and well-known work ethic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBD_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-SBD-175">[173]</a></sup> Jordan often used perceived slights to fuel his performances. Sportswriter <a href="/wiki/Wright_Thompson" title="Wright Thompson">Wright Thompson</a> described him as "a killer, in the Darwinian sense of the word, immediately sensing and attacking someone's weakest spot."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-left_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-left-3">[3]</a></sup> As the Bulls organization built the franchise around Jordan, management had to trade away players who were not "tough enough" to compete with him in practice. To help improve his defense, he spent extra hours studying film of opponents. On offense, he relied more upon instinct and improvization at game time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Noted as a durable player, Jordan did not miss four or more games while active for a full season from 1986–87 to 2001–02, when he injured his right knee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-br-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[175]</a></sup> Of the 15 seasons Jordan was in the NBA, he played all 82 regular season games nine times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-br-31">[29]</a></sup> Jordan has frequently cited <a href="/wiki/David_Thompson_(basketball)" title="David Thompson (basketball)">David Thompson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Davis_(basketball)" title="Walter Davis (basketball)">Walter Davis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jerry_West" title="Jerry West">Jerry West</a> as influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup> Confirmed at the start of his career, and possibly later on, Jordan had a special "Love of the Game Clause" written into his contract (unusual at the time) which allowed him to play basketball against anyone at any time, anywhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jordan had a versatile offensive game. He was capable of aggressively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penetration_(basketball)" title="Penetration (basketball)">driving</a> to the basket, as well as drawing <a href="/wiki/Personal_foul_(basketball)" title="Personal foul (basketball)">fouls</a> from his opponents at a high rate; his 8,772 <a href="/wiki/Free_throw" title="Free throw">free throw</a> attempts are the 11th-highest total in NBA history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup> As his career progressed, Jordan also developed the ability to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post_up" title="Post up">post up</a> his opponents and score with his trademark <a href="/wiki/Fadeaway" title="Fadeaway">fadeaway</a> <a href="/wiki/Jump_shot_(basketball)" title="Jump shot (basketball)">jump shot</a>, using his leaping ability to "fade away" from block attempts. According to <a href="/wiki/Hubie_Brown" title="Hubie Brown">Hubie Brown</a>, this move alone made him nearly unstoppable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[180]</a></sup> Despite media criticism as a "selfish" player early in his career, Jordan's 5.3 assists per game<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dbb_29-8"><a href="#cite_note-dbb-29">[27]</a></sup> also indicate his willingness to defer to his teammates. After shooting under 30% from <a href="/wiki/Three-point_field_goal" title="Three-point field goal">three-point</a> range in his first five seasons in the NBA, including a career-low 13% in the <a href="/wiki/1987%E2%80%9388_NBA_season" title="1987–88 NBA season">1987–88</a> season, Jordan improved to a career-high 50% in the <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_NBA_season" title="1994–95 NBA season">1994–95</a> season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dbb_29-9"><a href="#cite_note-dbb-29">[27]</a></sup> The three-point shot became more of a focus of his game from 1994–95 to 1996–97, when the NBA shortened its three-point line from 23 ft 9 in (7.24 m) to 22 ft (6.7 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup> His three-point field-goal percentages ranged from 35% to 43% in seasons in which he attempted at least 230 three-pointers between 1989–90 and 1996–97.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-br-31">[29]</a></sup> For a guard, Jordan was also a good rebounder (6.2 per game).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dbb_29-10"><a href="#cite_note-dbb-29">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1988, Jordan was honored with the NBA's <a href="/wiki/NBA_Defensive_Player_of_the_Year_Award" title="NBA Defensive Player of the Year Award">Defensive Player of the Year Award</a> and became the first NBA player to win both the Defensive Player of the Year and <a href="/wiki/NBA_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="NBA Most Valuable Player Award">Most Valuable Player</a> awards in a career. In addition, he set both seasonal and career records for <a href="/wiki/Block_(basketball)" title="Block (basketball)">blocked shots</a> by a guard,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[182]</a></sup> and combined this with his ball-thieving ability to become a standout defensive player. He ranks third in NBA history in total <a href="/wiki/Steal_(basketball)" title="Steal (basketball)">steals</a> with 2,514, trailing <a href="/wiki/John_Stockton" title="John Stockton">John Stockton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jason_Kidd" title="Jason Kidd">Jason Kidd</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jerry_West" title="Jerry West">Jerry West</a> often stated that he was more impressed with Jordan's defensive contributions than his offensive ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnnsi_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnnsi-186">[184]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Doc_Rivers" title="Doc Rivers">Doc Rivers</a> declared Jordan "...the best superstar defender in the history of the game."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>He was also known to have strong eyesight; broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Al_Michaels" title="Al Michaels">Al Michaels</a> said that he was able to read baseball box scores on a 27-inch (69 cm) television clearly from about 50 feet (15 m) away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[186]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/2001_NBA_Finals" title="2001 NBA Finals">2001 NBA Finals</a>, Phil Jackson compared Jordan's dominance to Shaquille O'Neal, stating "Michael would get fouled on every play and still have to play through it and just clear himself for shots instead and would rise to that occasion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[187]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_career_achievements_by_Michael_Jordan" title="List of career achievements by Michael Jordan">List of career achievements by Michael Jordan</a></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks vcard hlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f8f9fa;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;border: 4px double #E31E27;"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;font-size: 90%; padding: 0; line-height: 150%;"><table><tbody><tr>

</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;text-align:center;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r992953826">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .navbox .navbar{display:block;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .navbox-title .navbar{float:left;text-align:left;margin-right:0.5em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Michael_Jordan_series" title="Template:Michael Jordan series"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Michael_Jordan_series&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Template talk:Michael Jordan series (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Michael_Jordan_series&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Jordan's talent was clear from his first NBA season; by November he was being compared to <a href="/wiki/Julius_Erving" title="Julius Erving">Julius Erving</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gross_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gross-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garden_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Garden-39">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Larry_Bird" title="Larry Bird">Larry Bird</a> said that rookie Jordan was the best player he had ever seen, and that he was "one of a kind", and comparable to <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Gretzky" title="Wayne Gretzky">Wayne Gretzky</a> as an athlete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verdi19850207_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-verdi19850207-190">[188]</a></sup> In his first game in <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a> against the New York Knicks, Jordan received a near minute-long standing ovation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garden_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-Garden-39">[37]</a></sup> After establishing the single game playoff record of 63 points against the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Celtics" title="Boston Celtics">Boston Celtics</a> on April 20, 1986, Bird described him as "God disguised as Michael Jordan".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbadisguised_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-nbadisguised-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jordan <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_annual_scoring_leaders" title="List of National Basketball Association annual scoring leaders">led the NBA in scoring</a> in 10 seasons (NBA record) and tied <a href="/wiki/Wilt_Chamberlain" title="Wilt Chamberlain">Wilt Chamberlain</a>'s record of seven consecutive scoring titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-17"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup> He was also a fixture of the <a href="/wiki/NBA_All-Defensive_Team" title="NBA All-Defensive Team">NBA All-Defensive First Team</a>, making the roster nine times (NBA record shared with <a href="/wiki/Gary_Payton" title="Gary Payton">Gary Payton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Garnett" title="Kevin Garnett">Kevin Garnett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kobe_Bryant" title="Kobe Bryant">Kobe Bryant</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup> Jordan also holds the top career regular season and playoff scoring averages of 30.1 and 33.4 points per game, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[190]</a></sup> By 1998, the season of his Finals-winning shot against the Jazz, he was well known throughout the league as a clutch performer. In the regular season, Jordan was the Bulls' primary threat in the final seconds of a close game and in the playoffs; he would always ask for the ball at crunch time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[191]</a></sup> Jordan's total of 5,987 points in the playoffs is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_career_playoff_scoring_leaders" title="List of National Basketball Association career playoff scoring leaders">second-highest in NBA history</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[192]</a></sup> He retired with 32,292 points in regular season play,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESPN_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESPN-195">[193]</a></sup> placing him fifth on the <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_career_scoring_leaders" title="List of National Basketball Association career scoring leaders">NBA's all-time scoring list</a> behind <a href="/wiki/Kareem_Abdul-Jabbar" title="Kareem Abdul-Jabbar">Kareem Abdul-Jabbar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Malone" title="Karl Malone">Karl Malone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kobe_Bryant" title="Kobe Bryant">Kobe Bryant</a>, and <a href="/wiki/LeBron_James" title="LeBron James">LeBron James</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESPN_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-ESPN-195">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>With five regular-season MVPs (tied for second place with <a href="/wiki/Bill_Russell" title="Bill Russell">Bill Russell</a>—only <a href="/wiki/Kareem_Abdul-Jabbar" title="Kareem Abdul-Jabbar">Kareem Abdul-Jabbar</a> has won more, with six), six Finals MVPs (NBA record), and three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NBA_All-Star_Game_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="NBA All-Star Game Most Valuable Player Award">All-Star Game MVPs</a>, Jordan is the most decorated player in NBA history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_31-9"><a href="#cite_note-br-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[194]</a></sup> Jordan finished among the top three in regular-season MVP voting 10 times,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_31-10"><a href="#cite_note-br-31">[29]</a></sup> and was named one of the <a href="/wiki/50_Greatest_Players_in_NBA_History" title="50 Greatest Players in NBA History">50 Greatest Players in NBA History</a> in 1996. He is one of only seven players in history to win an NCAA championship, an NBA championship, and an Olympic gold medal (doing so twice with the 1984 and 1992 U.S. men's basketball teams).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoops_TC_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoops_TC-197">[195]</a></sup> Since 1976, the year of the NBA's <a href="/wiki/ABA%E2%80%93NBA_merger" title="ABA–NBA merger">merger</a> with the <a href="/wiki/American_Basketball_Association" title="American Basketball Association">American Basketball Association</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup> Jordan and Pippen are the only two players to win six NBA Finals playing for one team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[197]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/List_of_NBA_All-Star_vote_leaders" title="List of NBA All-Star vote leaders">All-Star Game fan ballot</a>, Jordan received the most votes nine times, more than any other player.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r887775652">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"There's Michael Jordan and then there is the rest of us."</div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—<a href="/wiki/Magic_Johnson" title="Magic Johnson">Magic Johnson</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-18"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Many of Jordan's contemporaries have said that Jordan is the greatest basketball player of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnnsi_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-cnnsi-186">[184]</a></sup> In 1999, an <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a> survey of journalists, athletes and other sports figures ranked Jordan the greatest North American athlete of the 20th century, above such luminaries as <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-century_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-century-201">[199]</a></sup> Jordan placed second to Babe Ruth in the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a>' December 1999 list of 20th century athletes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[200]</a></sup> In addition, the Associated Press voted him the greatest basketball player of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup> Jordan has also appeared on the front cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a></i> a record 50 times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[202]</a></sup> In the September 1996 issue of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sport_(US_magazine)" title="Sport (US magazine)">Sport</a></i>, which was the publication's 50th-anniversary issue, Jordan was named the greatest athlete of the past 50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sport_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sport-205">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Jordan's athletic leaping ability, highlighted in his back-to-back <a href="/wiki/Slam_Dunk_Contest" title="Slam Dunk Contest">Slam Dunk Contest</a> championships in 1987 and 1988, is credited by many people with having influenced a generation of young players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoop_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoop-206">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[205]</a></sup> Several NBA players—including <a href="/wiki/LeBron_James" title="LeBron James">LeBron James</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dwyane_Wade" title="Dwyane Wade">Dwyane Wade</a>—have stated that they considered Jordan their role model while they were growing up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup> In addition, commentators have dubbed a number of next-generation players "the next Michael Jordan" upon their entry to the NBA, including <a href="/wiki/Penny_Hardaway" title="Penny Hardaway">Penny Hardaway</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grant_Hill_(basketball)" title="Grant Hill (basketball)">Grant Hill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allen_Iverson" title="Allen Iverson">Allen Iverson</a>, Bryant, James, <a href="/wiki/Vince_Carter" title="Vince Carter">Vince Carter</a>, and Wade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[210]</a></sup> Although Jordan was a well-rounded player, his "Air Jordan" image is also often credited with inadvertently decreasing the jump shooting skills, defense, and fundamentals of young players,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoop_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoop-206">[204]</a></sup> a fact Jordan himself has lamented. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I think it was the exposure of Michael Jordan; the marketing of Michael Jordan. Everything was marketed towards the things that people wanted to see, which was scoring and dunking. That Michael Jordan still played defense and an all-around game, but it was never really publicized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoop_206-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hoop-206">[204]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>During his heyday, Jordan did much to increase the status of the game. Television ratings increased only during his time in the league.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cash_in_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-cash_in-213">[211]</a></sup> The popularity of the NBA in the U.S. declined after his last title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cash_in_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-cash_in-213">[211]</a></sup> As late as 2015, Finals ratings had not returned to the level reached during his last championship-winning season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Naismith_Memorial_Basketball_Hall_of_Fame" title="Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame">Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame</a> in <a href="/wiki/Springfield,_Massachusetts" title="Springfield, Massachusetts">Springfield, Massachusetts</a>, opened a Michael Jordan exhibit that contained items from his college and NBA careers, as well as from the <a href="/wiki/1992_United_States_men%27s_Olympic_basketball_team" title="1992 United States men's Olympic basketball team">1992 "Dream Team"</a>. The exhibit also has a <a href="/wiki/Baseball_glove" title="Baseball glove">batting glove</a> to signify Jordan's short career in Minor League Baseball.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[213]</a></sup> After Jordan received word of his acceptance into the Hall of Fame, he selected Class of 1996 member <a href="/wiki/David_Thompson_(basketball)" title="David Thompson (basketball)">David Thompson</a> to present him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[214]</a></sup> As Jordan would later explain during his induction speech in September 2009, when he was growing up in North Carolina, he was not a fan of the Tar Heels and greatly admired Thompson, who played at rival <a href="/wiki/NC_State_Wolfpack_men%27s_basketball" title="NC State Wolfpack men's basketball">North Carolina State</a>. In September, he was inducted into the Hall with several former Bulls teammates in attendance, including <a href="/wiki/Scottie_Pippen" title="Scottie Pippen">Scottie Pippen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Rodman" title="Dennis Rodman">Dennis Rodman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Oakley" title="Charles Oakley">Charles Oakley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ron_Harper" title="Ron Harper">Ron Harper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Kerr" title="Steve Kerr">Steve Kerr</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Toni_Kuko%C4%8D" title="Toni Kukoč">Toni Kukoč</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sam_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sam-217">[215]</a></sup> Two of Jordan's former coaches, Dean Smith and Doug Collins, were also among those present. His emotional reaction during his speech—when he began to cry—was captured by <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> photographer <a href="/wiki/Stephan_Savoia" title="Stephan Savoia">Stephan Savoia</a> and would later go viral on social media as the <a href="/wiki/Crying_Jordan" title="Crying Jordan">Crying Jordan</a> Internet meme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[217]</a></sup> In 2016, President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> honored Jordan with the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freedom_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freedom-220">[218]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michael_Jordan_and_Barack_Obama_at_the_White_House.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1349" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Michael_Jordan_and_Barack_Obama_at_the_White_House.jpg/170px-Michael_Jordan_and_Barack_Obama_at_the_White_House.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Michael_Jordan_and_Barack_Obama_at_the_White_House.jpg/255px-Michael_Jordan_and_Barack_Obama_at_the_White_House.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Michael_Jordan_and_Barack_Obama_at_the_White_House.jpg/340px-Michael_Jordan_and_Barack_Obama_at_the_White_House.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michael_Jordan_and_Barack_Obama_at_the_White_House.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jordan receiving the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> from President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a></div></div></div>
<p>Jordan is the fourth of five children. He has two older brothers, Larry Jordan and James R. Jordan Jr., one older sister, Deloris, and one younger sister, Roslyn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[220]</a></sup> James retired in 2006 as the <a href="/wiki/Sergeant_major" title="Sergeant major">Command Sergeant Major</a> of the <a href="/wiki/35th_Signal_Brigade_(United_States)" title="35th Signal Brigade (United States)">35th Signal Brigade</a> of the <a href="/wiki/XVIII_Airborne_Corps" title="XVIII Airborne Corps">XVIII Airborne Corps</a> in the U.S. Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[221]</a></sup> Jordan's nephew through Larry, Justin Jordan, played <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I" title="NCAA Division I">Division I</a> basketball at <a href="/wiki/UNC_Greensboro_Spartans_men%27s_basketball" title="UNC Greensboro Spartans men's basketball">UNC Greensboro</a> and is a scout for the Charlotte Hornets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[223]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jordan married Juanita Vanoy in September 1989. They had two sons, <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Jordan" title="Jeffrey Jordan">Jeffrey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Jordan" title="Marcus Jordan">Marcus</a>, and a daughter, Jasmine. The Jordans filed for divorce on January 4, 2002, citing irreconcilable differences, but reconciled shortly thereafter. They again filed for divorce and were granted a final decree of dissolution of marriage on December 29, 2006, commenting that the decision was made "mutually and amicably".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Divorce_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Divorce-226">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[225]</a></sup> It is reported that Juanita received a $168 million settlement (equivalent to $213 million in 2019), making it the largest celebrity divorce settlement on public record at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[227]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1991, Jordan purchased a lot in <a href="/wiki/Highland_Park,_Illinois" title="Highland Park, Illinois">Highland Park, Illinois</a>, on which he planned to build a 56,000 square-foot (5,200 m<sup>2</sup>) mansion. It was completed in 1995. He listed the mansion for sale in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mansion_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-mansion-230">[228]</a></sup> His two sons attended <a href="/wiki/Loyola_Academy" title="Loyola Academy">Loyola Academy</a>, a private <a href="/wiki/Catholic_school" title="Catholic school">Catholic school</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wilmette,_Illinois" title="Wilmette, Illinois">Wilmette, Illinois</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup> Jeffrey graduated in 2007 and played his first collegiate basketball game for the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Fighting_Illini_men%27s_basketball" title="Illinois Fighting Illini men's basketball">University of Illinois</a> on November 11, 2007. After two seasons, he left the Illinois basketball team in 2009. He later rejoined the team for a third season,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[231]</a></sup> then received a release to transfer to the <a href="/wiki/UCF_Knights_men%27s_basketball" title="UCF Knights men's basketball">University of Central Florida</a>, where Marcus was attending.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[233]</a></sup> Marcus transferred to <a href="/wiki/Whitney_M._Young_Magnet_High_School" title="Whitney M. Young Magnet High School">Whitney Young High School</a> after his sophomore year at Loyola Academy and graduated in 2009. He began attending UCF in the fall of 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[234]</a></sup> and played three seasons of basketball for the school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[235]</a></sup>
</p><p>On July 21, 2006, a judge in <a href="/wiki/Cook_County,_Illinois" title="Cook County, Illinois">Cook County, Illinois</a>, determined that Jordan did not owe his alleged former lover Karla Knafel $5 million in a breach of contract claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KaK_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-KaK-238">[236]</a></sup> Jordan had allegedly paid Knafel $250,000 to keep their relationship a secret.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap2_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap2-239">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday1_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday1-240">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whiotv_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-whiotv-241">[239]</a></sup> Knafel claimed Jordan promised her $5 million for remaining silent and agreeing not to file a <a href="/wiki/Paternity_law" title="Paternity law">paternity suit</a> after Knafel learned she was pregnant in 1991. A <a href="/wiki/Genealogical_DNA_test" title="Genealogical DNA test">DNA test</a> showed Jordan was not the father of the child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KaK_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-KaK-238">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jordan proposed to his longtime girlfriend, Cuban-American model <a href="/wiki/Yvette_Prieto" title="Yvette Prieto">Yvette Prieto</a>, on Christmas 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[240]</a></sup> and they were married on April 27, 2013, at <a href="/wiki/Bethesda-by-the-Sea" title="Bethesda-by-the-Sea">Bethesda-by-the-Sea</a> Episcopal Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[242]</a></sup> It was announced on November 30, 2013, that the two were expecting their first child together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[244]</a></sup> On February 11, 2014, Prieto gave birth to identical twin daughters named Victoria and Ysabel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[245]</a></sup> Jordan became a grandfather in 2019 when his daughter Jasmine gave birth to a son, whose father is professional basketball player <a href="/wiki/Rakeem_Christmas" title="Rakeem Christmas">Rakeem Christmas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[246]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_figure_and_business_interests">Media figure and business interests</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Endorsements">Endorsements</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_The_U.S._Army_-_www.Army.mil_(273).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1506" data-file-width="1205" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Flickr_-_The_U.S._Army_-_www.Army.mil_%28273%29.jpg/170px-Flickr_-_The_U.S._Army_-_www.Army.mil_%28273%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Flickr_-_The_U.S._Army_-_www.Army.mil_%28273%29.jpg/255px-Flickr_-_The_U.S._Army_-_www.Army.mil_%28273%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Flickr_-_The_U.S._Army_-_www.Army.mil_%28273%29.jpg/340px-Flickr_-_The_U.S._Army_-_www.Army.mil_%28273%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_The_U.S._Army_-_www.Army.mil_(273).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jordan in 2008</div></div></div>
<p>Jordan is one of the most marketed sports figures in history. He has been a major spokesman for such brands as <a href="/wiki/Nike,_Inc." title="Nike, Inc.">Nike</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet" title="Chevrolet">Chevrolet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gatorade" title="Gatorade">Gatorade</a>, <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald's">McDonald's</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ball_Park_Franks" title="Ball Park Franks">Ball Park Franks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spectrum_Brands" title="Spectrum Brands">Rayovac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheaties" title="Wheaties">Wheaties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanes" title="Hanes">Hanes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/MCI_Communications" title="MCI Communications">MCI</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rov_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-rov-249">[247]</a></sup> Jordan has had a long relationship with Gatorade, appearing in over 20 commercials for the company since 1991, including the "<a href="/wiki/Be_Like_Mike" title="Be Like Mike">Be Like Mike</a>" commercials in which a song was sung by children wishing to be like Jordan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rov_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-rov-249">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[248]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nike created a signature shoe for Jordan, called the <a href="/wiki/Air_Jordan" title="Air Jordan">Air Jordan</a>, in 1984.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[249]</a></sup> One of Jordan's more popular commercials for the shoe involved <a href="/wiki/Spike_Lee" title="Spike Lee">Spike Lee</a> playing the part of <a href="/wiki/Mars_Blackmon" title="Mars Blackmon">Mars Blackmon</a>. In the commercials Lee, as Blackmon, attempted to find the source of Jordan's abilities and became convinced that "it's gotta be the shoes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rov_249-2"><a href="#cite_note-rov-249">[247]</a></sup> The hype and demand for the shoes even brought on a spate of "shoe-jackings" where people were robbed of their sneakers at gunpoint. Subsequently, Nike spun off the Jordan line into its own division named the "Jordan Brand". The company features an impressive list of athletes and celebrities as endorsers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup> The brand has also sponsored college sports programs such as those of <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Tar_Heels" title="North Carolina Tar Heels">North Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/California_Golden_Bears" title="California Golden Bears">California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_Hoyas" title="Georgetown Hoyas">Georgetown</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marquette_Golden_Eagles" title="Marquette Golden Eagles">Marquette</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jordan also has been associated with the <a href="/wiki/Looney_Tunes" title="Looney Tunes">Looney Tunes</a> cartoon characters. A Nike commercial shown during 1992's <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XXVI" title="Super Bowl XXVI">Super Bowl XXVI</a> featured Jordan and <a href="/wiki/Bugs_Bunny" title="Bugs Bunny">Bugs Bunny</a> playing basketball.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup> The Super Bowl commercial inspired the 1996 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Films_with_live_action_and_animation" title="Films with live action and animation">live action/animated</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Jam" title="Space Jam">Space Jam</a></i>, which starred Jordan and Bugs in a fictional story set during the former's first retirement from basketball.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porter_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-256">[254]</a></sup> They have subsequently appeared together in several commercials for MCI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porter_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-256">[254]</a></sup> Jordan also made an appearance in the music video for <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson" title="Michael Jackson">Michael Jackson</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Jam_(song)" title="Jam (song)">Jam</a>" (1992).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jordan's yearly income from the endorsements is estimated to be over $40 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ausport_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-ausport-258">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[257]</a></sup> In addition, when Jordan's power at the ticket gates was at its highest point, the Bulls regularly sold out both their home and road games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[258]</a></sup> Due to this, Jordan set records in player salary by signing annual contracts worth in excess of US$30 million per season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[259]</a></sup> An academic study found that Jordan's first NBA comeback resulted in an increase in the market capitalization of his client firms of more than $1 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[260]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of Jordan's endorsement deals, including his first deal with Nike, were engineered by his agent, <a href="/wiki/David_Falk" title="David Falk">David Falk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-executiveofyear_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-executiveofyear-263">[261]</a></sup> Jordan has described Falk as "the best at what he does" and that "marketing-wise, he's great. He's the one who came up with the concept of 'Air Jordan.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[262]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_ventures">Business ventures</span></h3>
<p>In June 2010, Jordan was ranked by <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> magazine as the 20th-most powerful celebrity in the world with $55 million earned between June 2009 and June 2010. According to the <i>Forbes</i> article, Jordan Brand generates $1 billion in sales for Nike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[263]</a></sup> In June 2014, Jordan was named the first NBA player to become a billionaire, after he increased his stake in the Charlotte Hornets from 80% to 89.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[265]</a></sup> On January 20, 2015, Jordan was honored with the <i>Charlotte Business Journal'</i>s Business Person of the Year for 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[266]</a></sup> In 2017, he became a part owner of the <a href="/wiki/Miami_Marlins" title="Miami Marlins">Miami Marlins</a> of <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[267]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Forbes</i> designated Jordan as the athlete with the highest career earnings in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[268]</a></sup> From his Jordan Brand income and endorsements, Jordan's 2015 income was an estimated $110 million, the most of any retired athlete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[269]</a></sup> As of 2020<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Michael_Jordan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, his net worth is estimated at $2.1 billion by <i>Forbes</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[270]</a></sup> making him the <a href="/wiki/Black_billionaires" title="Black billionaires">fourth-richest African-American</a>, behind <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Smith_(investor)" title="Robert F. Smith (investor)">Robert F. Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Steward" title="David Steward">David Steward</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oprah_Winfrey" title="Oprah Winfrey">Oprah Winfrey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[271]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jordan co-owns an automotive group which bears his name. The company has a <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">Nissan</a> dealership in <a href="/wiki/Durham,_North_Carolina" title="Durham, North Carolina">Durham, North Carolina</a>, acquired in 1990,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[272]</a></sup> and formerly had a <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Motor_Company" title="Lincoln Motor Company">Lincoln</a>–<a href="/wiki/Mercury_(automobile)" title="Mercury (automobile)">Mercury</a> dealership from 1995 until its closure in June 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dealership_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-dealership-275">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[274]</a></sup> The company also owned a Nissan franchise in <a href="/wiki/Glen_Burnie,_Maryland" title="Glen Burnie, Maryland">Glen Burnie, Maryland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dealership_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-dealership-275">[273]</a></sup> The restaurant industry is another business interest of Jordan's. His restaurants include a <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jordan%27s_Steakhouse" title="Michael Jordan's Steakhouse">steakhouse</a> in New York City's <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Terminal" title="Grand Central Terminal">Grand Central Terminal</a>, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[275]</a></sup> Jordan is the majority investor in a golf course, Grove XXIII, under construction in <a href="/wiki/Hobe_Sound,_Florida" title="Hobe Sound, Florida">Hobe Sound, Florida</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[276]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2020, Jordan became an investor and advisor for <a href="/wiki/DraftKings" title="DraftKings">DraftKings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[277]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philanthropy">Philanthropy</span></h3>
<p>From 2001 to 2014, Jordan hosted an annual golf tournament, the Michael Jordan Celebrity Invitational, that raised money for various charities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philanthropic_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-philanthropic-280">[278]</a></sup> In 2006, Jordan and his wife Juanita pledged $5 million to Chicago's <a href="/wiki/Hales_Franciscan_High_School" title="Hales Franciscan High School">Hales Franciscan High School</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[279]</a></sup> The Jordan Brand has made donations to <a href="/wiki/Habitat_for_Humanity" title="Habitat for Humanity">Habitat for Humanity</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Boys_%26_Girls_Clubs_of_America" title="Boys &amp; Girls Clubs of America">Boys &amp; Girls Clubs of America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[280]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Make-A-Wish_Foundation" title="Make-A-Wish Foundation">Make-A-Wish Foundation</a> named Jordan its Chief Wish Ambassador in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philanthropic_280-1"><a href="#cite_note-philanthropic-280">[278]</a></sup> Five years later, he granted his 200th wish for the organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[281]</a></sup> As of 2019, he has raised more than $5 million for the Make-A-Wish Foundation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philanthropic_280-2"><a href="#cite_note-philanthropic-280">[278]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, Jordan donated a settlement of undisclosed size from a lawsuit against supermarkets that had used his name without permission to 23 different Chicago charities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[282]</a></sup> Jordan funded two Novant Health Michael Jordan Family Clinics in <a href="/wiki/Charlotte,_North_Carolina" title="Charlotte, North Carolina">Charlotte, North Carolina</a>, in 2017 by giving $7 million, the biggest donation he had made at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[283]</a></sup> One year later, after <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Florence" title="Hurricane Florence">Hurricane Florence</a> damaged parts of North Carolina, including his former hometown of Wilmington, Jordan donated $2 million to relief efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[284]</a></sup> He gave $1 million to aid the Bahamas' recovery following <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Dorian" title="Hurricane Dorian">Hurricane Dorian</a> in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[285]</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 5, 2020, in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/George_Floyd_protests" title="George Floyd protests">protests</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Killing_of_George_Floyd" title="Killing of George Floyd">killing of George Floyd</a>, Jordan and his brand announced in a joint statement that they will be donating $100 million over the next 10 years to organizations dedicated to "ensuring racial equality, social justice and greater access to education."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[286]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_and_television">Film and television</span></h3>
<p>Jordan played himself in the 1996 comedy film <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Jam" title="Space Jam">Space Jam</a></i>. The film was a box office success, making $230 million worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[287]</a></sup> although the reviews were mixed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[288]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2000, Jordan was the subject of an <a href="/wiki/IMAX" title="IMAX">IMAX</a> documentary about his career with the Chicago Bulls, especially the <a href="/wiki/1998_NBA_playoffs" title="1998 NBA playoffs">1998 championship season</a>, entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Michael_Jordan_to_the_Max" title="Michael Jordan to the Max">Michael Jordan to the Max</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[289]</a></sup> Two decades later, the same period of Jordan's life was covered in much greater and more personal detail by the <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" title="Emmy Award">Emmy Award</a>-winning <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Dance_(miniseries)" title="The Last Dance (miniseries)">The Last Dance</a></i>, a 10-part TV documentary which debuted on <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a> in April and May 2020. <i>The Last Dance</i> relied heavily on about 500 hours of candid film of Jordan's and his teammates' off-court activities which an <a href="/wiki/NBA_Entertainment" title="NBA Entertainment">NBA Entertainment</a> crew had shot over the course of the <a href="/wiki/1997%E2%80%9398_NBA_season" title="1997–98 NBA season">1997–98 NBA season</a> for use in a documentary.  The project was delayed for many years because Jordan had not yet given his permission for the footage to be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[291]</a></sup> He was interviewed at three homes associated with the production and did not want cameras in his home or on his plane as "there are certain aspects of his life that he wants to keep private", according to director Jason Hehir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[292]</a></sup> Jordan granted rapper <a href="/wiki/Travis_Scott" title="Travis Scott">Travis Scott</a> permission to film a music video for his single <a href="/wiki/Franchise_(song)" title="Franchise (song)">"Franchise"</a> at his home in <a href="/wiki/Highland_Park,_Illinois" title="Highland Park, Illinois">Highland Park, Illinois</a> "without hesitation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[293]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Books">Books</span></h3>
<p>Jordan has authored several books focusing on his life, basketball career, and world view.
</p>
<ul><li><i>Rare Air: Michael on Michael</i>, with Mark Vancil and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Iooss" title="Walter Iooss">Walter Iooss</a> (Harper San Francisco, 1993).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[294]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[295]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>I Can't Accept Not Trying: Michael Jordan on the Pursuit of Excellence</i>, with Mark Vancil and Sandro Miller (Harper San Francisco, 1994).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[296]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>For the Love of the Game: My Story</i>, with Mark Vancil (Crown Publishers, 1998).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[297]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Driven from Within</i>, with Mark Vancil (Atria Books, 2005).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[298]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honors">Awards and honors</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JordanSmithWorthy2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2464" data-file-width="1648" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/JordanSmithWorthy2.jpg/170px-JordanSmithWorthy2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/JordanSmithWorthy2.jpg/255px-JordanSmithWorthy2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/JordanSmithWorthy2.jpg/340px-JordanSmithWorthy2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JordanSmithWorthy2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_Worthy" title="James Worthy">James Worthy</a>, Jordan, and <a href="/wiki/Dean_Smith" title="Dean Smith">Dean Smith</a> in 2007 at a <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Tar_Heels_men%27s_basketball" title="North Carolina Tar Heels men's basketball">North Carolina Tar Heels men's basketball</a> game honoring the 1957 and 1982 men's basketball teams.</div></div></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="College">College</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I_Men%27s_Basketball_Tournament" title="NCAA Division I Men's Basketball Tournament">NCAA national championship</a> – <a href="/wiki/1982_NCAA_Division_I_Men%27s_Basketball_Tournament" title="1982 NCAA Division I Men's Basketball Tournament">1981–82</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honors_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-honors-301">[299]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Coast_Conference" title="Atlantic Coast Conference">ACC Freshman of the Year</a> – 1981–82<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACC_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACC-302">[300]</a></sup></li>
<li>Two-time Consensus <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Men%27s_Basketball_All-Americans" title="NCAA Men's Basketball All-Americans">NCAA All-American First Team</a> – 1982–83, 1983–84<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACC_302-1"><a href="#cite_note-ACC-302">[300]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ACC_Men%27s_Basketball_Player_of_the_Year" title="ACC Men's Basketball Player of the Year">ACC Men's Basketball Player of the Year</a> – 1983–84<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACC_302-2"><a href="#cite_note-ACC-302">[300]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oscar_Robertson_Trophy" title="Oscar Robertson Trophy">USBWA College Player of the Year</a> – 1983–84<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[301]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Naismith_College_Player_of_the_Year" title="Naismith College Player of the Year">Naismith College Player of the Year</a> – 1983–84<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-19"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adolph_Rupp_Trophy" title="Adolph Rupp Trophy">Adolph Rupp Trophy</a> – 1983–84<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[302]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_R._Wooden_Award" title="John R. Wooden Award">John R. Wooden Award</a> – 1983–84<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-20"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li>Number 23 <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Tar_Heels_men%27s_basketball#Retired_numbers" title="North Carolina Tar Heels men's basketball">retired by the North Carolina Tar Heels</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[303]</a></sup></li></ul>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NBA">NBA</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r998391716" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li>Six-time <a href="/wiki/NBA_Finals" title="NBA Finals">NBA champion</a> – <a href="/wiki/1991_NBA_Finals" title="1991 NBA Finals">1991</a>, <a href="/wiki/1992_NBA_Finals" title="1992 NBA Finals">1992</a>, <a href="/wiki/1993_NBA_Finals" title="1993 NBA Finals">1993</a>, <a href="/wiki/1996_NBA_Finals" title="1996 NBA Finals">1996</a>, <a href="/wiki/1997_NBA_Finals" title="1997 NBA Finals">1997</a>, <a href="/wiki/1998_NBA_Finals" title="1998 NBA Finals">1998</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-21"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li>Six-time <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_Russell_NBA_Finals_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Bill Russell NBA Finals Most Valuable Player Award">NBA Finals MVP</a> – <a href="/wiki/1991_NBA_Finals" title="1991 NBA Finals">1991</a>, <a href="/wiki/1992_NBA_Finals" title="1992 NBA Finals">1992</a>, <a href="/wiki/1993_NBA_Finals" title="1993 NBA Finals">1993</a>, <a href="/wiki/1996_NBA_Finals" title="1996 NBA Finals">1996</a>, <a href="/wiki/1997_NBA_Finals" title="1997 NBA Finals">1997</a>, <a href="/wiki/1998_NBA_Finals" title="1998 NBA Finals">1998</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_31-11"><a href="#cite_note-br-31">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>Five-time <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="National Basketball Association Most Valuable Player Award">NBA MVP</a> – <a href="/wiki/1987%E2%80%9388_NBA_season" title="1987–88 NBA season">1988</a>, <a href="/wiki/1990%E2%80%9391_NBA_season" title="1990–91 NBA season">1991</a>, <a href="/wiki/1991%E2%80%9392_NBA_season" title="1991–92 NBA season">1992</a>, <a href="/wiki/1995%E2%80%9396_NBA_season" title="1995–96 NBA season">1996</a>, <a href="/wiki/1997%E2%80%9398_NBA_season" title="1997–98 NBA season">1998</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-22"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/NBA_Defensive_Player_of_the_Year_Award" title="NBA Defensive Player of the Year Award">NBA Defensive Player of the Year</a> – <a href="/wiki/1987%E2%80%9388_NBA_season" title="1987–88 NBA season">1987–88</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honors_301-1"><a href="#cite_note-honors-301">[299]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/NBA_Rookie_of_the_Year_Award" title="NBA Rookie of the Year Award">NBA Rookie of the Year</a> – <a href="/wiki/1984%E2%80%9385_NBA_season" title="1984–85 NBA season">1984–85</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-23"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li>10-time <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_annual_scoring_leaders" title="List of National Basketball Association annual scoring leaders">NBA scoring leader</a> – 1987–1993, 1996–1998<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_31-12"><a href="#cite_note-br-31">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>3-time <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Basketball_Association_annual_steals_leaders" title="List of National Basketball Association annual steals leaders">NBA steals leader</a> – 1988, 1990, 1993<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_31-13"><a href="#cite_note-br-31">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>14-time <a href="/wiki/NBA_All-Star_Game" title="NBA All-Star Game">NBA All-Star</a> – 1985–1993, 1996–1998, 2002, 2003<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_31-14"><a href="#cite_note-br-31">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>Three-time <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NBA_All-Star_Game_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="NBA All-Star Game Most Valuable Player Award">NBA All-Star Game MVP</a> – <a href="/wiki/1988_NBA_All-Star_Game" title="1988 NBA All-Star Game">1988</a>, <a href="/wiki/1996_NBA_All-Star_Game" title="1996 NBA All-Star Game">1996</a>, <a href="/wiki/1998_NBA_All-Star_Game" title="1998 NBA All-Star Game">1998</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br_31-15"><a href="#cite_note-br-31">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>11-time <a href="/wiki/All-NBA_Team" title="All-NBA Team">All-NBA Team</a>:</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>First team – 1987–1993, 1996–1998<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-24"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li>Second team – 1985<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-25"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li>Nine-time <a href="/wiki/NBA_All-Defensive_Team" title="NBA All-Defensive Team">NBA All-Defensive First Team</a> – 1988–1993, 1996–1998<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-26"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li>Two-time <a href="/wiki/Slam_Dunk_Contest#NBA_Slam_Dunk_Contest_champions" title="Slam Dunk Contest">NBA Slam Dunk Contest champion</a> – 1987, 1988<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-27"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li>Two-time <a href="/wiki/IBM_Award" title="IBM Award">IBM Award</a> winner – 1985, 1989<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honors_301-2"><a href="#cite_note-honors-301">[299]</a></sup></li>
<li>Named one of the <a href="/wiki/50_Greatest_Players_in_NBA_History" title="50 Greatest Players in NBA History">50 Greatest Players in NBA History</a> in 1996<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-28"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li>Number 23 <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Bulls#Retired_numbers_and_honorees" title="Chicago Bulls">retired by the Chicago Bulls</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-numbers_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-numbers-306">[304]</a></sup></li>
<li>Number 23 <a href="/wiki/Miami_Heat#Retired_numbers" title="Miami Heat">retired by the Miami Heat</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-numbers_306-1"><a href="#cite_note-numbers-306">[304]</a></sup></li></ul>
</div>

<ul><li>class of 2015 – individual<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[307]</a></sup></li>

<li>Three-time <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press_Athlete_of_the_Year" title="Associated Press Athlete of the Year">Associated Press Athlete of the Year</a> – 1991, 1992, 1993<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[310]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated_Sportsperson_of_the_Year" title="Sports Illustrated Sportsperson of the Year"><i>Sports Illustrated</i> Sportsperson of the Year</a> – 1991<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[311]</a></sup></li>
<li>Section of <a href="/wiki/Madison_Street_(Chicago)" title="Madison Street (Chicago)">Madison Street</a> in Chicago renamed Michael Jordan Drive – 1994<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[312]</a></sup></li>
<li>Ranked No.1 by <i><a href="/wiki/Slam_(magazine)" title="Slam (magazine)">Slam</a></i> magazine's Top 50 Players of All-Time<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[313]</a></sup></li>
<li>Ranked No.1 by <i>ESPN <a href="/wiki/SportsCentury" title="SportsCentury">SportsCentury</a>'</i>s Top North American Athletes of the 20th century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-century_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-century-201">[199]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_members_of_the_North_Carolina_Sports_Hall_of_Fame" title="List of members of the North Carolina Sports Hall of Fame">North Carolina Sports Hall of Fame</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[314]</a></sup></li>
<li>1997 <a href="/wiki/Marca_Leyenda" title="Marca Leyenda">Marca Leyenda</a> winner<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[315]</a></sup></li>
<li>10-time <a href="/wiki/ESPY_Award" title="ESPY Award">ESPY Award</a> winner (in various categories)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[316]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Jordan_statue" title="Michael Jordan statue">Statue</a> in front of the <a href="/wiki/United_Center" title="United Center">United Center</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[317]</a></sup></li>
<li>2016 <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freedom_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freedom-220">[218]</a></sup></li></ul>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jordan's weight fluctuated from 195 lb (88 kg) to 218 lb (99 kg) during the course of his professional career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-left_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-left-3">[3]</a></sup> However, his listed weight was 216 lb (98 kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbah_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-nbah-5">[5]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jordan wore a nameless No. 12 jersey in a February 14, 1990 game against the <a href="/wiki/Orlando_Magic" title="Orlando Magic">Orlando Magic</a> because his No. 23 jersey had been stolen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> Jordan scored 49 points, setting a franchise record for players wearing that jersey number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
